Simhat Torah Honorees

Special Events

The High Holiday season reaches its joyous climax on Simhat Torah, the last day of the festival of Sukkot. Because
of our perennial love of the Torah, it has been referred to as Israel’s “bride”. On Simhat Torah, we bring toa close the
annual cycle of reading the Torah and immediatley begin anew. An ancient traditition evolved of honoring worthy
men by calling them to the Torah for the last reading. Hatan Torah, and the beginning portion, Hatan Bereshit. In
our synagogue tradition, we also honor the contributions made by a “Woman of Valor’.

Milton Berman

Hatan Torah

Mea to i
_ Berman was born in
a small village in
Russia in September
1921. A year later,
_ when the Cossacks
came and killed all
of their neighbors,
| Milton's father
decided that it was
time. for them: to
leave. The family made their way to
Germany and then on to Grinnell, lowa.
where they lived in a small house built on
his uncle’s scrap yard.

After graduating from high school in
1940, Milton, joined the Navy and was
stationed in Jacksonville. While attending
a USO dance at the Temple, he met his
lovely bride, Edith. They were married in
1944, and moved back to Iowa for 5 months.
During that time, Milton decided that he
really liked Jacksonville, so he and Edith
moved back. Having grown up in a scrap
yard, it was only natural for Milton to open
one of his own. He and his brother Harry ran
it together for many years.The business grew
and so did Milton’s family.

Soon. son (Charles. was ‘born,
followed by son Eric, and then son Alan.
Shortly after that, Milton and Edith wanted
to adopt a baby girl, but instead, gave infant
Randy a home. Randy was a special baby.
He, like Edith, had a heart condition, and
Milton and Edith wanted to give him not
only a home, but also a chance to live.

Unfortunately, Randy passed away at
the age of 17, and Alan at the age of 42.
Milton and Edith have kept the memory of
both boys alive by them generous
contributions to the Center and other Jewish
organizations. For many years, they have
sponsored a major part of our Performing
Artists Series in memory of their sons.

Milton Berman is a quiet man, hard
working and devoted husband, father and
grandfather. His inner strength and faith in
God has helped him turn personal adversity
into generosity that now benefits a whole
community.

Yasher Koach to Milton, Edith and
their entire family.

 

Michael DuBow
Hatan Bereshit

Michael has
the pedrenee on a
community leader.
His father Laurie
DuBow is a past
Center president,
board member,
SUs€ Cre Set wf
businessman and
noted philanthropist.
Michael was raised to follow in his father’s
footsteps.

A graduate of the Bolles School, he
went on to receive a degree in accounting
from Tulane University and an MBA from
the University of Florida. He worked in
New York before moving back to
Jacksonville and entering the family
business.

Over the next few years, Michael
began to establish himself as a dedicated
volunteer and civic leader. He currently
serves on the Boards of Directors for the
Jacksonville Jewish Federation, the
Jacksonville Jewish Foundation and the
city’s Museum of Science and History
(MOSH), as well as our synagogue Board.
He has been part of Leadership Jacksonville
and, last year, received the Federation’s
Ilene Selevan Young Leadership award.

In 1998, Michael became the
Center’s Vice President of Education, a
position he still holds. In addition , he chairs
the Education Executive Committee which
oversees the development of policies and
programs to benefit the students, faculty and
parents of all our Center Schools.

Michael is dedicated to providing the
finest Jewish education for the children of
Jacksonville. He has been the guiding force
behind the Solomon Schechter Day School
“ipo stock” program that allows the
community to “invest in the future of Jewish
education.” Michael was instrumental in
obtaining computers for the students of our
Preschool and Hebrew Schools.

The future of our synagogue is secure
with leaders like Michael DuBow.

Sharing this honor was Michael’s
wife Helen and their children, Sophie and
Charlie.

 

Etta Drashin
Woman of Valor

A native of
Jacksonville, Etta is
a third generation
Center member and
a product of the
Center schools. A
graduate of Wolfson
High School and the
University of
Georgia, Etta has put

eat her education and
teaching skills to good use as a volunteer
remedial teacher for New American
children in the Solomon Schechter Day
School and as a beginning Hebrew teacher
for over 70 adults.

An ongoing learning Jew, Etta’s
distinguishing trait is her love of Judaism.
She has participated in the adult B’nai
Mitzvah class, attended the Wagner
Institute at the Jewish Theological
Seminary, and regularly attends Rabbi
Gaffney’s Sisterhood Seminars, Torah
Study and Talmud classes. She has also
been accepted into “Kolot B’K’dush,” the
Women’s League for Conservative Judaism
national corps of women skilled in reading
Torah. She was also among the special
group of people who helped organize the
Men’s Club New American B’nai Mitzvah.

Etta has been honored for her many
volunteer activities. She received the
Jewish Family & Community Services
volunteer of the year award for her work
helping to resettle Jews from the former
Soviet Union and was honored at a
Women’s League Spring Conference with
the Sisterhood leadership award. Etta sits
on many committees as a Center Board
member and Center Sisterhood board
member. A life member of Hadassah and
National Council of Jewish Women, Etta
has served as co-President of Tikva group
of Hadassah, and Past President of the
Jewish Women’s Bowling League.

She has co-chaired the Center’s
Performing Artist Series and has helped
form several of the Center’s existing
Havurot. Her community activities include,
presidency of DuPont Junior High School's
PTA and a vice-presidency of Wolfson
High School’s PTA.

We extend mazel tov to Etta’s
daughters, Heidi and Sandy, and her
mother, Charlotte Fialkow who share in
this honor.

students throughout the community from 8th - 12th grades.

Education, Jewish Ethics and Values, Jewish History, Judaic
Studies and text. Classes meet Wednesday evenings from
7-9 p.m. For more information visit our website at

Rabbi Gaffney Tribute Dinner

i

 
 

a

a

Jacksonville
Jewish Center

Centennial

Celebration
190° = 2000
eae

Record crowd paid
tribute to Rabbi
David Gatfiney

More than 500 people were in attendance at
this special event to honor Rabbi David
Gaffney, Sunday evening, June 10

 

It was an evening of nostalgia, an evening of fond remembrances, an evening of high humor, an unforgettable
evening for Rabbi Gaffney, his family and all of us who were privileged to be there. Linda and Laurie DuBow were
consumate chairpersons of the event. Margo’s dinner was superb. Each speaker, from the youngest to the oldest, gave
tribute with their individual account of their personal association with our Rabbi, lauding his accomplishments, his
spiritual leadership and their friendship. The slide retrospective, narrated by Laurie DuBow, was a tour de force of epic
proportions. It was an apt tribute to 29 years of devoted service. It was an evening to express our wishes of a future with
much happiness and “God Speed” from all of us, and from all of those who made this evening possible.

Jacksonville

Centennial
Celebration
190 001

 
 

SEPTEMBER 6, 1908 CORNER STONE WAS LAID:
When the cornerstone was laid on September 6, 1908,
the members of the building committee enclosed a
statement signed in Hebrew by all of the members of
the building committee that concluded with these words:

“With great pleasure, through
diligent work by the Building
Committee and the members of
our society, have now succeeded
in building this synagogue. Today
we have the honor of laying the
cornerstone of First Orthodox
Synagogue in Jacksonville.”

1909 WE MOVED IN:

The building was completed and
dedicated in 1909. The building
was designed to accommodate the necessity of a school
facility as well as a sanctuary. The lower floor was
devoted entirely to the school, with classrooms and a
large assembly hall. The main floor housed the
sanctuary and in Orthodox style there was a gallery
reserved for women.

1919 RABBI, DR. STEIN ARRIVES:

The congregation’s first ordained Rabbi, Dr. Salo Stein
arrived in 1919. He instituted a late Friday evening
Service, and mid-week lectures were introduced for a
more complete program for the synagogue. During
Rabbi Stein’s tenure, a Sunday Religious School
program was instituted, and Troop 14 Boy Scout Troop
was formed in 1919. In 1921 Rabbi Press succeeded
Dr. Stein and remained for the next two years.

1922 DAUGHTERS OF ISRAEL:

Rabbi Press organized the Daughters of Israel in April
1922 led by its first president Mrs. Harry Finkelstein.
This organization is now known as the Center
Sisterhood. It’s goals of financial aid and various social
and philanthropic activities continue on as major
endeavors of the Center Sisterhood.

1926 RABBI SAMUEL BENJAMIN:

One of the most significant changes in the history of
the congregation took place on April 1, 1926. Harry
Gendzier played a key role in the selection of Dr. Samuel
Benjamin, a graduate of the Jewish Theological

1909 -

 

A Centenial Chronicle

 

loz]

Seminary, as rabbi of the congregation. Benjamin
represented the American educated type of rabbi,
holding a degree from Brown University, a Dr. of
Jurisprudence from New York University and ordination
from the Jewish Theological Seminary of America. With
the arrival of Rabbi Benjamin in 1926 a tremendous
evolution took place in the life of the
congregation. He was an excellent
educator, a respected scholar, and a
| dynamic speaker. Shortly after his
‘ arrival, the mode of worship changed
to a more Conservative pattern.
Cantor Aaron Edgar was added to the
staff. The community immediately
felt the influence of Rabbi Benjamin.
He launched an intensive campaign
to create a Jewish community center.
The independence of the proposed
center was emphasized by Rabbi’s statement that “the
Jacksonville Jewish Center is not to be the institution of
particular congregation, group or society. It is to
represent the entire Jewish community on the broad
principle of a living American Judaism.”

1927 JACKSONVILLE JEWISH CENTER
CHARTER:

On January 27, 1927 a charter was issued for the new
Jacksonville Jewish Center. Harry Finkelstein became
president of the new organization. Lionel D. Joel
became first Vice- President, and Max Rubin became
second vice-president. Joseph Witten, Jacob Lapinsky,
David Moskovitz, Paul Newman and Moses Feldman,
previously of B’ Nai Israel were included on the Board.
Newcomers to the leadership were Louis Bucholtz,
Herman Bloom, Phillip Bork, S.D. Kramer and Jack
Rosenberg.

DECEMBER 1928 THE NEW BUILDING WAS
READY AT THIRD AND SILVER:

On January 8, 1928, the first part of the dedication took
place in a procession carrying the Holy Scrolls from
Duval and Jefferson Streets to the Ark in the new
synagogue. That evening a delicious banquet featured
an address by Florida Governor John W. Martin. In the
program Rabbi Benjamin stressed the goal that the new
edifice would succeed only if the spirit of Judaism was
in it. Described in detail were the new sanctuary with
its balcony, the auditorium, the kitchen, daily prayer

Hadassah

Hadassah will be celebrating the most exciting event
of the decade, Hadassah’s 90th Anniversary. Our Chapter
will be celebrating this significant milestone in February.

Judaism teaches that it is not enough to avoid iniquity
— we must actively pursue righteousness and justice at
home, in the, community, and in the world. In this tradition,
Hadassah was founded in 1912 by Henrietta Szold to bring
public health services to pre-state Israel. Today, more than
300,000 American women support our network of Israel
projects in addition to our volunteer work at home. Across
the US, Hadassah promotes health education, community
Service, and Jewish education and research. Hadassah
advocates on issues ranging from Israel-US relations to
First Amendment protections to women’s rights and health
concerns.

One by one, we must each take responsibility for
transforming hopes, promises, and echoes into deeds.
Jacksonville Hadassah invites your participation in one of
our five groups: Hannah Senesch, Shir Li, Tikva, Nurse’s
& HCP Council and Yamit.

For information please contact any of our
presidents:

Janis Dorman
Leah Solomon
Shoshana Hass

Community Organizations

 

National Council of Jewish Women

The National Council of Jewish Women (NCJW) is
a volunteer organization, inspired by Jewish values, that
works through a program of research, education, advocacy,
and community service to improve the quality of life for
women, children and families, and strives to ensure
individual rights and freedoms for all.

The Jacksonville section of NCJW was established
in 1939, and is currently involved in such programs as:

1. Organizing forums for candidates for local offices to
present their views to the Jewish community;

2. Bringing kosher meals, as well as a caring word, to
homebound Jewish people;

3. Organizing advocacy on state and federal issues
involving woman, children and families;

4. Providing a Hanukkah celebration at Wolfson’s Children’s
Hospital, bringing entertainment and gifts to patients;

5. Registering voters as part of a national “Promote the
Vote” campaign;

6. Providing entertainment and refreshments to Mt. Carmel
residents;

7. Participating in Israeli society through a variety of
education-oriented projects;

8. Recognizing women who have made great contributions to
our community, through our annual Women in Power
Luncheon;

9. Continued support of programs which we helped start, and
which now function independently, such as Guardian Ad
Litem, Home Instruction Program for Preschool Youngsters
and PACE.

For information, contact Gloria Einstein or
the NCJW Message Line — 443-2820

Jacksonville
Jewish Center

Centennial

Celebration
190d =. 200d

Troop 14 continued the
scouting tradition at the
Jacksonville Jewish Center with
another year of outdoor fun,
adventure and activities in 2000-
2001, Bob Pleéct served as
Scoutmaster, and was assisted by
Assistant Scoutmasters Louis
Schwartz and Billy Wilensky.
Youth leadership was provided by Jared Fleet, as Junior
Assistant Scoutmaster.

This year, the troop went on campouts to Fort
Clinch State Park, Paynes Prairie State Preserve, and
Ginnie Springs. At Fort Clinch, they completed a 12
mile Historic Trail hike and at Ginnie Springs, they
kayaked and snorkeled. The troop also bunked and went
horse back riding at Diamond D Stables.

Other activities the scouts enjoyed included tubing
on the Ichetucknee River, golfing at Palm Valley Golf
Course and a trip to the World Golf Village. The scouts
once again competed for the famed “Silver Cup of
Oratory”.

 

Justin Jackrel became an Eagle Scout.

Mention the Center Yearbook when Patronizing Our Advertisers

 

Scouting

 

Boy Scout Troop 14

Troop 14 participated in
Saturday morning Shabbat services
at River Garden and at the Center
on Scout Shabbat. Once again, the
scouts purchased food for the JFCS
Food Bank at Thanksgiving.

A lot of scouts made strides
in advancement this year. Justin
Jackrel became the most recent
member of Troop 14 to earn Boy Scouting’s highest
honor, the rank of Eagle. Thirteen scouts attended
summer camp with Troop 14 at Camp Rainey Mountain
in Clayton, Georgia. They were led by Assistant
Scoutmaster Louis Schwartz and Steve Meisel.

Troop 14 looks forward to continuing its proud
tradition of providing Jewish youth with a Boy Scouting
experience. If you enjoy camping, sports, a variety of
outdoor activities, and are between the ages of 11 and
18, we invite you to come to our meetings at the Center
on Thursday nights. The troop also welcomes
participation from adults interested in providing
volunteer assistance.

For more information call Bob Fleet at:
(H) 268-2746 or (W)730-8103.

Introduction to Silent Amidah

No one is as remote as you, Lord our God,

yet no one is closer to us, Fatherly Ruler.

You are beyond the confines of all creation,

yet forever present in every moment.

Our deepest thought cannot grasp Your infinity

nor understand the shadow of Your glory’s reflection.

But the whispered prayer,
rising from the depths of despair,
the unvoiced cry of mute needs,
the soft sigh, draining the broken heart of its sorrow,

the silent supplication if a thin, stilled voice —

All these are like great shofar blasts,
tearing the fabric of Your sphere’s peace,

touching Your endlessness wherever You are.

Suddenly You, whom the utmost heavens cannot contain,

deign to dwell in each single searching heart.

May the words of my mouth and the meditations of my heart
find favor in Yours eyes. Amen

— Rabbi Immanuel Lubliner

To all our friends, a year of peace and good health, and to children everywhere, we pledge our efforts to improve the world.

5, The Jewish Theological Seminary of America

The Seminary is the academic and religious center of the worldwide movement of Conservative Judaism.
Believing that its message for the New Year 5762 is of compelling importance, we have arranged for its publication.

Religious Committee

Chairman — David Schmerer

We are entering a new era at the Jacksonville
Jewish Center and the Religious Committee will play a
central role in how our congregation will develop
religiously. This is no small undertaking for we must
blend the traditions of the past century with the challenges
of a new Rabbi and Hazzan and the current trends in
Conservative Judaism.

Our Committee is about to become arguably the
hardest working group in our Congregation. We have
critical oversight on all areas of religious practice,
ceremony and decorum and a substantial interest in the
areas of education and youth. The Religious Committee
is dedicated to ensure that all facets of the religious life
of our Congregation are spiritual, meaningful and central
to each and every congregant.

It is equally important that all of us demonstrate
our visible commitment to Conservative Judaism and to
our congregation by attending Shabbat and Holiday
Services and attending at least one daily Shacarit or
Mincha/Maariv Minyan each week. We should also make
every attempt to participate in adult education programs
and to join our Sisterhood and Men’s Club to show our
support for those valuable groups. It is critically
important, especially if you have young children, to
practice Jewish ritual in your home like lighting Shabbat
candles, doing Havdalah and keeping Kashrut. I realize
that there are varying levels of observance outside of the
Center so wherever you currently are on the observance
scale try moving it up a notch.

Any member of the Religious Committee stands
ready to help you achieve these goals. All you need to do
is ask.

Committees

 

Henry Kramer Library & Resource Center
Minnie Schreiber and May Goldie Stillman, Chairs

In 1955 Roselyn Selber and her co-workers, with
the help of USY’ers moved books to what was to become
the first library. Charlote Glossman was the first librarian.

In 1959 the first library committee was formed
under Joe Zell then Joe Becker, and to the present chaired
by Minnie Schrieber. About this time the library acquired a
collection of books that belonged to Rabbi Sanders Tofield
that were to become the ““Tofield Memorial Library”’.

In 1976 came the big move to Crown Point Road
and once again, with the help of the USY’ers, books were
packed and unpacked in our new synagogue. Mrs Henry
Kramer with the assistance of Edwin Hugo furnished and
equipped the expanded library which is now known as the
Henry Kramer Library and Resource Center.

The library consists of several entities, such as the
Solomon Schechter Day School Library, a modern comput-
erized library with a trained Librarian. The Tofield Read-
ing Room, a depository of books, information, and materi-
als of Jewish interest.

The “Rita Gendzier Judaica Collection” was dedi-
cated in 1980 in memory of Rita Gendzier’s devotion to the
library.

This maginificent library is the result of 50 years

of devotion and assistance of countless synagogue mem-
bers to whom we are eternally grateful.

Library Committee

 

 
 

Members:
Blanche Slott, Roselyn Selber
Svetlana Goldiner, Barbara Pakula

Staff Assistants:
Stephanie Shacter, Debbie Shacter

Camp Ramah Palmer and Ramah Darom

The Jacksonville Jewish Center 1s very fortunate to have a large representation
of youngsters participating in the Camp Ramah programs both in New England and
the new Ramah Darom in Georgia. Ramah, an arm of the Conservative Movement,
offers a wonderful summer opportunity for informal enrichment and meaningful
education.

their Jewish heritage. In addition to

Samuel & Esther Galinsky Educational Programs — PTA

 

Day School/PreSchool PTA

The PTA of the Solomon Schechter Day School and the Center Pre-school is proud to
support our children and their education. We work closely with our teachers and our
administrative staff to enhance the programming of our school. By joining the PTA and
volunteering to help in any capacity, we not only enrich our children, but we also make
lasting contributions to our schools and make many wonderful friendships.

Our major fundraising efforts include the magazine sale, book fair, Passover candy
sale, and Library birthday party. Last year’s fundraising was so successful that we purchased
amyriad of new equipment and instructional enhancements for our schools, including Science
Lab supplies and equipment, a new Ellison Lettering machine, supplies for the music program,
the Accellerated Reading program, and Reading manipulatives for kindergarten through third
grades, a Library Laser scanner, a cot for the sick room, a costume closet for school plays,
and a brochure for the pre-school. We also contributed funds to each classroom and purchased
books for the library.

Some of the educational and enrichment programs we provide are: Holiday observances,
such as Shabbat boxes, weekly Challah orders, helping with celebrations of Rosh Hashanah,
Chanukah, Purim and Passover, as well as Kaleidoscope Cultural Arts Day, Red Ribbon
Week for Drug Awareness, Vision Screening, Library Assistants, and Mystery Reader.

We appreciate your commitment to the PTA. Whether you volunteer your time or give
your financial support, the result is always a successful educational experience for our children.

L’ Shana Tovah,
—Sue Levine

 

2001-2002 Solomon Schechter Day School Teachers and Staff

As a young
Jacksonville : :
Jewish Center boy growing up in
Cone Newark,New Jersey
Celebration
1902 = 2001 and Richmond,
a

Virginia, | have

distinct and very
clear memories of attending synagogue
on Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur. |
am not sure if I truly understood all that
was taking place but there was one part
of the service on Rosh Hashanah that
needed no explanation, the sounding

of the shofar.

The shofar service stirred me
and left a lasting impression. What a
strange and awe-inspiring custom! For
me it was the highlight of the service and until this day I
always feel a bit cheated when the first day of Rosh
Hashanah falls on a Shabbat and the shofar is not sounded.
Over the years I have had the privilege, on occasions, to
act as Baal Tekiah and no matter how many times I have
performed this mitzvah it never fails to inspire and move
me. Certainly, much has been written and said about the
meaning and significance of the shofar. My colleague,
Rabbi Samuel Chiel of Boston, offers the following story
with a unique perspective: “The story is about a lifeguard
in California who was also serving as a camp counselor.
The young man was explaining to his class what the shofar
was all about. He said, ‘Just as when you wander too far
out into the deep water and I blow my whistle as a warning
that you are getting into danger, so we blow the shofar
once a year to remind us that we shouldn’t wander too far
into deep water.”

The shofar demands that we get out of the deep
water and come back home. It is a signal to every Jew
that the New Year is at hand and that we must begin
looking inward in order to do true and meaningful
Teshuvah (repentance). The shofar also reminds us that
in ancient times it was used to announce important
communal events. This year, as we hear the notes of the
shofar, it not only resonates with a call to changes as
individuals but also to announce a milestone in the life of
the Jacksonville Jewish Center. This year we celebrate the
one hundredth anniversary of our Center and its many
contributions to the lives of countless thousands of Jews.
When we rise to experience the sounds of the shofar this
Yom Toy, each person present in the sanctuary will recall
how the Center has impacted their lives. For me this year
the shofar will not look into the past but will look towards
the future and begin what I hope and pray will be a long
and fruitful relationship with each member of the
Jacksonville Jewish Center.

 

Rabbi Stephen Grundfast

Rabbi Grundfast’s Message

I begin my tenure with a
deep sense of humility and
thankfulness. One of the most
important words in the Machzor is
‘Hineni’. This is the first word of the
prayer intoned by the Hazzan just
before the Musaf Amidah on the
Yamim Noraim-the Days of Awe.
When the Hazzan sings this prayer
he is ready to take on the awesome
task and responsibility of praying on
behalf of the congregation. In some
synagogues the Hazzan begins this
prayer at the rear of the sanctuary and
slowly walks to the bima singing the
words of Hineni. He approaches the
reading table with respect, honor and
a bit of trepidation for the great task
at hand. One of the lines he reads goes as follows, “V’lo
yehee shoom meechshole b’tefilati”-’And may my prayer
be without faltering”.

As we get to know each other better I, like the
High Holiday Hazzan, approach my new position as your
rabbi with respect, honor and a bit of trepidation.
Together we will listen to the sounds of the shofar for
inspiration as we begin a new chapter in the wonderful
history of our congregation. While it will take some time
for our relationship to grow and mature, I say “Hineni “,
here I am, ready for the challenges and opportunities that
I know the future will bring. Working together we can
begin building for the next one hundred years of
excellence and serious Jewish commitment.

On behalf of my wife, Judy, son, Joseph and my
daughter, Rachel, may I wish each of you a Shanah Tovah
u’Metukah-A-Good and Sweet New Year!

 

- Rabbi Stephen Grundfast

—— Membership ia Daily Minyan Captains

Traditionally, public worship requires a minyan, a
quorum of ten men. Only in the presence of a minyan may
the Torah be read or the Kaddish prayer be recited.

Our dedicated minyan captains ensure that there is a
minyan twice every single day of the year. It is often a hard
and thankless job. It requires hours of phoning to coordinate
attendance.

 

It is because of them that you can recite kaddish

Setting for Your Oldest Traditions

Our beautiful, riverfront location is Jacksonville’s newest setting for
your oldest, most treasured traditions. We offer the perfect space,
whether you’re planning a small family gathering for ten or a
celebration for 4,000. Consider an outdoor event on one of our
unique terraces, providing a beautiful view of the city skyline and the
St. Johns River. It’s the perfect backdrop for any event. With 966 guest
rooms, including 21 suites, your out-of-town guests have many
comfortable options for their stay. And local guests will find our
central downtown location easily accessible with convenient valet or
self-parking available. —

For over 53 years, the Center Choir has been enhancing services on O Natalie Saud David
the High Holidays and Shavuot with moving liturgical music. Over the renee) ee ee ie e

Center Havurot

Havurah makes it possible for a family, couple or
single person, to make a personal connection within the
larger framework of the JJC.

The Center has a growing number of havurot whose
activities are varied according to the members’ wishes, and
may include education, discussion of Jewish issues, sharing
holiday and lifestyle celebrations, or just getting together
on a regular basis to socialize.

For many of our members, particularly those who
have relocated to Jacksonville, the havurah provides an
extended family and support group. The Center would be
happy to facilitate the formation of a new havurah and for

 

; : ; Children in their “Havurah” tee shirts gathered on Sukkot at the home
those who are interested in doing this, call the Center at of Barbara and Eliot Safer, in 1988. They still do!

292-1000.

Jewish Singles

The Jacksonville Jewish Singles calendar has been filled with activity. This year in addition to the 20 to 40
something group, a 40 to 60ish group has been getting together. To add to the mix , some special events are on the
calendar for a group from 35 to 45. These groups have enjoyed dinners and deserts at homes and at restaruants, as
well as attending concerts and art shows and have even taken an occasional hike.

A schedule of events is mailed every 3 months and is listed in the Jacksonville Jewish News plus serveral of
the local synagogue bulletins. There are no membership dues or requirements, other than being single, Jewish and
a desire to meet other single Jewish adults. Many great friendships have been formed and a few marriages have
taken place.

For more information, or to be put on the mailing list, call Rhoda at the Center office 292-1000.
